        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/jacs.7b12267"><meta property="og:title" content="Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/jacs.7b12267.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Alkali ions can be plated dendrite-free into a liquid alkali-metal anode. Commercialized Na–S battery technology operates above 300 °C. A low-cost Na–K alloy is liquid at 25 °C from 9.2 to 58.2 wt% of sodium; sodium and/or potassium can be plated dendrite-free in the liquid range at room temperature. The co-existence of two alkali metals in an anode raises a question: whether the liquid Na–K alloy acts as a Na or a K anode. Here we show the alkali-metal that is stripped from the liquid Na–K anode is dependent on the preference of the cathode host. It acts as the anode of a sodium rechargeable cell if the cathode host structure selectively accepts only Na+ ions; as the anode of a potassium rechargeable cell if the cathode accepts K+ ions in preference to Na+ ions. This dual-anode behavior means the liquid Na–K alkali-alloy can be applied as a dendrite-free anode in Na-metal batteries as well as K-metal batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/jacs.7b12267.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6f001684f775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6f001684f775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '38e10ecf-9907-4470-a455-3e07d3237cb6'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6f001684f775c-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6f001684f775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'JACS';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/JACS/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/JACS/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/jacs.7b12267&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/jacs.7b12267" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="90f6f001684f775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "jacsat";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Cathode Dependence of Liquid-Alloy Na–K Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.7b12267", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3292%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLeigang%2BXue%252C%2BHongcai%2BGao%252C%2BYutao%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D140%26issueNum%3D9%26contentID%3Djacs.7b12267%26title%3DCathode%2BDependence%2Bof%2BLiquid-Alloy%2BNa%25E2%2580%2593K%2BAnodes%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0002-7863%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Djacsat%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3298%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2018" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/medium/ja-2017-12267f_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.7b12267&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Alkali ions can be plated dendrite-free into a liquid alkali-metal anode. Commercialized Na–S battery technology operates above 300 °C. A low-cost Na–K alloy is liquid at 25 °C from 9.2 to 58.2 wt% of sodium; sodium and/or potassium can be plated dendrite-free in the liquid range at room temperature. The co-existence of two alkali metals in an anode raises a question: whether the liquid Na–K alloy acts as a Na or a K anode. Here we show the alkali-metal that is stripped from the liquid Na–K anode is dependent on the preference of the cathode host. It acts as the anode of a sodium rechargeable cell if the cathode host structure selectively accepts only Na<sup>+</sup> ions; as the anode of a potassium rechargeable cell if the cathode accepts K<sup>+</sup> ions in preference to Na<sup>+</sup> ions. This dual-anode behavior means the liquid Na–K alkali-alloy can be applied as a dendrite-free anode in Na-metal batteries as well as K-metal batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3292%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLeigang%2BXue%252C%2BHongcai%2BGao%252C%2BYutao%2BLi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D140%26issueNum%3D9%26contentID%3Djacs.7b12267%26title%3DCathode%2BDependence%2Bof%2BLiquid-Alloy%2BNa%25E2%2580%2593K%2BAnodes%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0002-7863%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Djacsat%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3298%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrolytes" class="article__tags__link">Electrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292492&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Liquids" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Liquids" class="article__tags__link">Liquids</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292006&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Potassium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Potassium" class="article__tags__link">Potassium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Alkali-metal rechargeable batteries have a metallic Li, Na, or K anode. A solid alkali-metal anode forms dendrites during charge. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> The dendrites can grow across the electrolyte to the cathode to create an internal short-circuit with incendiary consequences. This problem has limited commercial lithium–metal batteries to disposable primary cells that only allow discharge; they are found in watches, cameras and implantable medical devices.</div><div class="NLM_p">So far, rechargeable alkali-metal batteries can only be commercially realized by using a molten alkali-metal anode at high temperature in a Na–S battery; no dendrites form on a liquid anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 4 to 7">(4−7)</a> A solid electrolyte separates the two liquid electrodes of a Na–S cell. Although the melting point of sodium is only 97.8 °C, the liquid-sodium batteries operate over 300 °C due to the low conductivity of the solid electrolytes. The high operating temperature gives rise to problems of thermal management, corrosion, safety, and also imposes stringent requirements on the rest of the battery components. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 and 8">(6,8)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Alkali metals are not liquid at room temperature, but a Na–K alloy is liquid from 9.2 to 58.2 wt% of sodium at 25 °C; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> both Na and K are earth-abundant and inexpensive. The liquid range is much wider than that of other room-temperature liquid alloys containing alkali metals (see the different phase diagrams in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>); <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 12 to 15">(12−15)</a> the alloy provides a high specific capacity of 1366 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> as a liquid sodium anode at 25 °C (see calculation in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Solid electrolytes that suffer from a low ionic conductivity are not necessary because the Na–K liquid alloy is immiscible with the standard liquid-carbonate electrolyte. Therefore, the liquid Na–K alloy resolves the dendrite problem while providing fast charge/discharge at room temperature.</div><div class="NLM_p last">As two neighbors in Group one of the periodic table, Na and K have similar physical and chemical properties, which raises the question: Does the Na–K alkali-alloy act as a sodium or a potassium anode? We show the answer depends on the cathode. A previous study of a cell having a liquid Na–K anode, a standard liquid-carbonate Na<sup>+</sup> electrolyte, and a Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode showed the cathode progressively transforms to K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> with cycling, indicating the Na–K alloy was working as a potassium anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> It met the normal expectation because potassium is more electropositive than sodium (K<sup>+</sup>/K = −2.93 V, Na<sup>+</sup>/Na = −2.71 V). However, here we report that the liquid Na–K alloy acts as a sodium anode when it works with cathodes that only accept Na<sup>+</sup> ions, such as the layered Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and NASICON-structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. It acts as a potassium anode when the cathode host prefers K<sup>+</sup> over Na<sup>+</sup> guest ions, such as Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>. The determinant of whether the liquid Na–K is a Na or a K anode is the energy gained by insertion of K<sup>+</sup> versus Na<sup>+</sup>; K<sup>+</sup> is preferred over Na<sup>+</sup> if K<sup>+</sup> insertion is more stable, but Na<sup>+</sup> if the K<sup>+</sup> cannot be inserted.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Liquid Na–K Anode Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Na–K liquid alloy was prepared by mixing 33.7 wt% sodium and 66.3 wt% potassium and shaking the container as previously reported. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> The liquid Na–K alloy was absorbed into a carbon paper at 420 °C and it remains an immobilized liquid in the paper down to room temperature. One gram of carbon could absorb about 5 g Na–K liquid. Carbon paper (GDL 20BA, areal weight = 85 g m<sup>–2</sup>) was bought from Ion Power. The carbon paper containing the Na–K liquid alloy was cut into disks for coin-cell tests. The liquid Na–K anode is excessive in all cells to make sure both Na and K are enough for the cathode hosts.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Cathode Preparations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The cathode powders, including Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> and K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> were prepared as reported previously. MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> was prepared by removal of Na<sup>+</sup> from Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> by charging; then it was assembled into new cells with fresh electrolyte and anode. The Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode contained 70% of cathode powder, 20% of carbon black, and 10% of CMC binder. Distilled water was used as solvent and the resultant slurry was thoroughly mixed and coated onto carbon-coated Al foil; the coating was dried under vacuum at 100 °C overnight. The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathodes consisted of 70% of cathode powder, 20% of carbon black, and 10% of PVDF binder. NMP was used as solvent for the slurry coating onto carbon-coated Al foil and the coating was dried under vacuum at 100 °C for 8 h. The Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode electrodes consisted of 60% cathode powder, 30% carbon black, and 10% CMC binder. Distilled water was used as solvent to create a slurry that was thoroughly mixed and coated onto carbon-coated Al foil; the coating was dried under air overnight.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrolyte Preparations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The composition of the Na<sup>+</sup> electrolyte is 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) containing 10 wt% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). The composition of the K<sup>+</sup> electrolyte was saturated KClO<sub>4</sub> in PC containing 10 wt% FEC.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Battery Assembly and Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p">CR2032 coin cells (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a) composed of a liquid Na–K anode, a liquid-carbonate electrolyte containing a NaClO<sub>4</sub> or KClO<sub>4</sub> salt, a glass-fiber separator, and different kinds of cathodes were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox. The cells were galvanostatically cycled at room temperature with a LAND battery testing system.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/medium/ja-2017-12267f_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Configuration and performance of a cell composed of a liquid Na–K anode, a liquid-carbonate Na<sup>+</sup> electrolyte, and a Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode. (a) Schematic of the cell structure. (b) Charge/discharge curves at different cycles. Plots of (c) differential capacity vs voltage; (d) Coulombic efficiency; and (e) cycling stability. The specific capacities were calculated on the basis of the mass of cathode material.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.7b12267&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Energy-Dispersive X-ray Spectroscopy (EDS) and X-ray Diffraction (XRD)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">After cycling (from the discharged state), the cells were disassembled in an argon-filled glovebox. The cycled cathode was rinsed thoroughly with diethyl carbonate (DEC) for EDS (Hitachi S5500 SEM/STEM equipped with an Oxford EDS system) and XRD (Rigaku MiniFlex 600 II) analysis. The cycled electrolyte salt in the glass-fiber separator was extracted with DEC solvent and then transferred to a grid of a scanning transmission electron microscope (STEM). After the solvent was evaporated, the electrolyte salt stuck to the holey carbon film of the STEM grid and was ready for elemental analysis. The initial electrolyte before cycling was directly dropped onto a STEM grid for EDS analysis as the control sample.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">A cell composed of a liquid Na–K anode, a standard liquid-carbonate electrolyte of 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in PC containing 10 wt% FEC, and a layered-structured Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode was assembled and tested. By taking advantage of the immiscibility between the liquid Na–K alloy and the organic liquid-carbonate electrolyte, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> a coin cell can be assembled with a traditional “sandwich” structure as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. The liquid alloy is immobilized in a carbon paper and the immiscible organic electrolyte is immobilized in a glass-fiber separator. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows charge/discharge curves of the cell over 500 cycles. Only a slight decrease of storage efficiency was observed due to an increase of polarization. Between 2.4 and 3.9 V, four pairs of peaks in d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> were observed; their central positions were at 3.15, 3.31, 3.60, and 3.66 V, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c), which are almost identical to those obtained with sodium half-cells. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> The cell exhibited a high average Coulombic efficiency of 99.64% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d) with little capacity fade over 500 cycles at 0.5 C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e); both the Coulombic efficiency and stability are highly improved compared with the previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 17, 21 and 22">(17,21,22)</a> It also exhibited an excellent rate performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">After cycling, the cell was disassembled, and the cycled cathode was rinsed with DEC and then analyzed by powder XRD and EDS. The XRD spectra of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a indicate the cycled cathode retains the P2-type layered structure and there is little structural change of the cathode particles after cycling. The EDS profile of the cathode after cycling shows no evidence of K<sup>+</sup> insertion or K<sup>+</sup> at the particle surface (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). Both the XRD and EDS reveal K<sup>+</sup> did not intercalate into the desodiated Na<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> host lattice.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/medium/ja-2017-12267f_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Cycled Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode and electrolyte. (a) XRD and (b) EDS patterns of the layered-structured Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode before and after the cycling with a liquid Na–K anode and a NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte. (c) STEM image of cycled electrolyte salt crystallized on a STEM grid and (d) its EDS spectroscopy. (e–g) Elemental mapping of Na, Cl, and O from the selected area in (c).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.7b12267&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cycled liquid electrolyte was also analyzed by EDS. In order to avoid interference from the complex components of the glass-fiber separator, which contained both Na and K (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), the cycled electrolyte salt in the glass-fiber separator was extracted with DEC solvent and then transferred to a grid of a STEM for elemental analysis (see the procedure illustration in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The initial electrolyte before cycling was directly dropped onto a STEM grid for EDS analysis as the control sample. After the solvent was evaporated, the electrolyte salt crystallized and stuck to the holey carbon film of the STEM grid as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c. Compared with the initial electrolyte before cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), the EDS profile of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d shows the salt after cycling is still NaClO<sub>4</sub> with no evidence of K<sup>+</sup> ions; the elemental distribution of Na, Cl, O shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e–g is consistent with the selected area in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c. EDS peaks of C, Cu, and Al were observed in all the electrolytes before and after cycling (compare <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5b</a>); they come from the STEM grid. F was only observed after cycling, indicating some side reactions such as SEI formation happens with the electrolyte additive FEC.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/medium/ja-2017-12267f_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. A liquid Na–K alkali-alloy anode acts as a sodium anode when working with cathodes that only allow Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. (a) Layered-structured Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode. (b) NASICON-structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode. Only Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the cathode host suppresses the K dissolution but promotes the Na dissolution from the liquid Na–K anode. The lattice structures of Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are illustrated based on their crystallographic parameters in previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.7b12267&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">These data show that the cell with the layered Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode acted like a simple sodium cell with a good cycle life; there was no evidence of K<sup>+</sup> extraction from the liquid Na–K alloy anode even though potassium is more electropositive than sodium; the standard potential difference between Na<sup>+</sup>/Na and K<sup>+</sup>/K has been reported as 0.3 V in PC. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> If K<sup>+</sup> had been preferentially removed from the liquid Na–K anode on discharge, but cannot intercalate into the cathode, analysis of the electrolyte after cycling should have shown evidence of K<sup>+</sup> in the electrolyte. The charge/discharge process is illustrated schematically in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. During charge, only Na<sup>+</sup> can be removed from the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode to the electrolyte and Na<sup>+</sup> from the electrolyte is introduced into the liquid Na–K anode. On discharge, since only Na<sup>+</sup> can be intercalated into the layered Na<sub>1/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode host, K<sup>+</sup> dissolution from the anode is suppressed and Na<sup>+</sup> dissolution from the liquid Na–K anode is promoted; only Na<sup>+</sup> is removed from the anode to the electrolyte and Na<sup>+</sup> returned to the cathode. The liquid Na–K acts as a pure sodium anode when working with the layered Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode that accepts only Na<sup>+</sup> ions.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to check the generality of this result, we tested a cell with another cathode, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, that does not allow K<sup>+</sup> insertion because of its small interstitial bottleneck of atomic separation of 2.50 Å (see its framework structure in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). In this cell, also, the liquid Na–K anode acted like a simple Na-metal anode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>), and the central position of the peaks in d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> was located at 3.38 V (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>), which is identical to that obtained with sodium half-cells. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> However, the as-prepared Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode did not show as excellent a cycling as the cell with the Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6c,d</a>), a capacity retention of 85% in 100 cycles was observed. The performance difference should be attributed to the cathodes themselves, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> should be further optimized to get better performance. The XRD (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>) and EDS data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>) of the cycled Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> indicated K<sup>+</sup> was not inserted into its framework as expected. As in the cell with the layered Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode, only EDS peaks of NaClO<sub>4</sub> and no peak of K were observed in the salt (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The liquid Na–K acts as a sodium anode when working with the NASICON-structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode, which also accepts only Na<sup>+</sup> ions, as is illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b.</div><div class="NLM_p">On the other hand, Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> is transformed into K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> after a few cycles with Na–K alloy as the anode and NaClO<sub>4</sub> as the initial electrolyte salt; the Na<sup>+</sup> electrolyte is transformed to a K<sup>+</sup> electrolyte on cycling. Once the cathode is transformed to K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, it remains unchanged after cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> This transformation can be monitored by the voltage increase with cycling, as shown in the charge/discharge curves of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a and d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>. The two pairs of peaks at low and high potentials can be assigned to low-spin Fe<sup>III</sup>/Fe<sup>II</sup> and high-spin Mn<sup>III</sup>/Mn<sup>II</sup> redox couples, respectively. With charge, a phase transformation occurs from a monoclinic phase to a cubic one over the first peak, and from cubic to tetragonal over the second peak. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 20, 26 and 27">(20,26,27)</a> When KClO<sub>4</sub> was used as the initial electrolyte salt, the transformation of Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> to K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> was accelerated. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows that the cell with a Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode and a KClO<sub>4</sub> liquid electrolyte acts as a potassium cell after the first charge; it also shows a good cycling stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> is not chemically prepared because of the instability of Mn<sup>3+</sup> in aqueous solution, but it can be formed by charging Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>; with a K<sup>+</sup> electrolyte, MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> behaves as a potassium cell from the beginning (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> has been prepared chemically, and the Na–K alloy also acts as a potassium anode with this cathode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> These data indicate that no matter whether the initial cathode is Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, or K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> and the initial electrolyte contains Na<sup>+</sup> or K<sup>+</sup>, the cells with the Na–K anode finally transform to potassium cells.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/medium/ja-2017-12267f_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. All cells with a MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>-based cathode and a Na–K anode transform into potassium batteries regardless of the initial cathodes and electrolytes. (a) For a (−) Na–K|Na<sup>+</sup>|Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> (+) cell, it behaves as a sodium battery at the beginning, but progressively transforms into a potassium battery after decades of cycles. The charge/discharge voltage increase with cycling is a sign of this transformation. (b) For a (−) Na–K|K<sup>+</sup>|Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> (+) cell, it transforms into a potassium battery just after the first charge. (c) For a (−) Na–K|K<sup>+</sup>|MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> (+) cell, it is a potassium battery at the beginning.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.7b12267&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> has a large octahedral interstitial space interconnected by open faces bordered by four C≡N anions (see the Na and K versions of MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> structure in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b); the narrowest C≡N interatomic distance is 4.77 Å in Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, which has been experimentally proven big enough for both Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> reversible insertion/extraction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref20 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref20 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 15, 20, 27 and 28">(15,20,27,28)</a> The recent observation that only K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> is precipitated from a mixture of Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, and Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub><sup>4–</sup> in aqueous solution on the addition of Mn<sup>2+</sup> shows that K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> is more stable than Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Their lattice structures show K<sup>+</sup> tends to form stronger bonds with C≡N than Na<sup>+</sup> (see the interatomic distance comparison in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>). It reveals the higher energy gain from inserting K<sup>+</sup> into the MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> host structure compared to inserting Na<sup>+</sup>. That is why the K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> has a higher energy density and discharge voltage than that of Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref20 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref20 ref27" aria-label="reference 15, 20 and 27">(15,20,27)</a> These experimental observations are supported by a first-principles study of FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>, that concluded the insertion of cations with larger ionic radius happens at higher voltages, and thus provides higher energy density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/medium/ja-2017-12267f_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. A liquid Na–K alkali-alloy anode acts as a potassium anode when working with a MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>-based cathode host. (a) With an initial Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode, K<sup>+</sup> stripped and is inserted into the cathode, transforming Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> into K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode, the cell reaction transforms from (a) to (b). The major cause of this transformation is the thermodynamic priority of the K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>. (b) With an initial K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode and initial K<sup>+</sup> electrolyte, it is always a potassium battery.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/asset/images/large/ja-2017-12267f_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.7b12267&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The charge/discharge processes of MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>-based cathodes are illustrated schematically in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>. During charge, only Na<sup>+</sup> can be removed from the Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode to the electrolyte and Na<sup>+</sup> from the electrolyte is introduced into the liquid Na–K anode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a). On discharge, since the MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> host prefers K<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup> dissolution from the liquid Na–K anode is promoted while the Na<sup>+</sup> dissolution from the anode is suppressed; K<sup>+</sup> is removed from the anode to the electrolyte and inserted into the cathode host, transforming Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> into K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub>. If K<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> is used as the initial cathode, K<sup>+</sup> is always the working ion, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b. The liquid Na–K acts as a pure potassium anode when working with the MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> host that prefers K<sup>+</sup> ions. If the cathode host cannot accept K<sup>+</sup> ions, the sodiated cathode is the more stable discharged cathode and the Na–K alloy acts as a Na anode. Where both Na<sup>+</sup> and K<sup>+</sup> can be inserted into the cathode, whether the liquid Na–K alloy acts as a Na or a K anode depends on the anode–cathode reaction that gives the highest reactive energy.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The Na–K alloy molten at room temperature is immiscible with the flammable liquid-carbonate electrolyte of the alkali-ion batteries. However, the alloy can be immobilized in a porous membrane that it wets. Therefore, it provides a way to plate dendrite-free sodium or potassium with the alloy at room-temperature from a flammable liquid electrolyte to provide a safe, fast charge/discharge Na-metal or K-metal anode for a rechargeable battery. Whether the alloy acts as a pure sodium or pure potassium anode depends on the relative stability of a discharged cathode into which K<sup>+</sup> versus Na<sup>+</sup> ions are inserted on discharge.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/jacs.7b12267" class="ext-link">10.1021/jacs.7b12267</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Figure S1, showing phase diagrams of room-temperature liquid alloys containing alkali metals; capacity calculation; and Figures S2–S10, showing rate capability; EDS of the glass fiber separator; illustration of the procedure used to extract the cycled electrolyte salt for element analysis; EDS of the initial NaClO<sub>4</sub> salt before cycling; performance of a cell composed of a liquid Na–K anode, a standard liquid-carbonate Na<sup>+</sup> electrolyte, and a NASICON-structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode; cycled Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode; cycled electrolyte salt in the cell with a NASICON-structured Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode; evolution of d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> with cycle number for a (−) Na–K|Na<sup>+</sup>|Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> (+) cell; and performance of a cell composed of a liquid Na–K anode, a standard liquid-carbonate K<sup>+</sup> electrolyte, and a Na<sub>2</sub>MnFe(CN)<sub>6</sub> cathode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.7b12267/suppl_file/ja7b12267_si_001.pdf">ja7b12267_si_001.pdf (1.4 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/jacs.7b12267" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium metal anodes for rechargeable batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Wu; Wang, Jiulin; Ding, Fei; Chen, Xilin; Nasybulin, Eduard; Zhang, Yaohui; Zhang, Ji-Guang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium (Li) metal is an ideal anode material for rechargeable batteries due to its extremely high theor. specific capacity (3860 mA h g-1), low d. (0.59 g cm-3) and the lowest neg. electrochem. potential (-3.040 V vs. the std. hydrogen electrode).  Unfortunately, uncontrollable dendritic Li growth and limited Coulombic efficiency during Li deposition/stripping inherent in these batteries have prevented their practical applications over the past 40 years.  With the emergence of post-Li-ion batteries, safe and efficient operation of Li metal anodes has become an enabling technol. which may det. the fate of several promising candidates for the next generation energy storage systems, including rechargeable Li-air batteries, Li-S batteries, and Li metal batteries which utilize intercalation compds. as cathodes.  In this paper, various factors that affect the morphol. and Coulombic efficiency of Li metal anodes have been analyzed.  Technologies utilized to characterize the morphol. of Li deposition and the results obtained by modeling of Li dendrite growth have also been reviewed.  Finally, recent development and urgent need in this field are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoNGXuk9rqhzrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj5OoSdnRoIFQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhsFaktL8%253D&amp;md5=3813f919eb7ca4218cfb562f54c9382a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.ssi.2008.01.070"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, Z.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The progress in the research and development of high temp. sodium batteries suggests that all-solid-state batteries with inorg. or polymer solid electrolytes are promising power sources for a wide range of applications due to their high thermal stability, reliability, long-cycle life and versatile geometries.  The electrolytes play a fundamental role in terms of current (power) d., the time stability, and the safety of batteries and, as a result, their continuous improvement and innovation are indeed crit. to success.  In fact, inorg. solid electrolytes pave the way for improving the cost-effective development of rechargeable sodium batteries.  This review describes a state-of-the-art overview of most of the Na+ conductors for use as electrolytes in sodium/sulfur and ZEBRA batteries.  The emphasis of this article is on inorg. solid electrolytes, esp., ceramic and glass-ceramic electrolytes as promising alternatives applicable to all solid-state batteries.  As part of a continuous effort to find new materials that operate at room temp. and moderate temps., NASICON electrolytes will also be considered.  Polymer electrolytes based on poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) are also very suitable for all solid-state batteries.  Hence, the review focuses on ion transport based on the obsd. cond., electrolyte prepn., safety and environmental impact.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNZo4WOUW9vLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj5OoSdnRoIFQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXivVGksLY%253D&amp;md5=818d05b78e3c16a03b3f86ba39c40f7e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1007/BF02647091"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Webster, D.</span></span> <i>Metall.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The effect of low-melting-point impurities on the properties of aluminum-lithium alloys</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Webster, Donald</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Metallurgical Transactions A:  Physical Metallurgy and Materials Science</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The effect of low m.p. impurities, such as Na and K, on the properties and microstructure of Al-Li alloys was investigated.  These impurities occurred as discrete lenticular particles along grain boundaries.  In underaged and peak-aged conditions, the impurity particles are liq. and the toughness and ductility of alloys in this condition were improved by lowering the test temp. below the f.p. of the impurity.  In the overaged condition, the Na and K occur as a solid phase and materials in this condition followed the pattern of most Al alloys and show a decrease in Charpy impact toughness as the testing temp. was lowered.  The addn. of Na and K to Al-Li alloys reduced toughness and ductility at room temp. (at ≤476 Na and ≤23 ppm K).  Low m.p. impurities had little effect on the toughness or ductility of high purity Al.  The detrimental effects of Na and K on toughness exist only in alloys contg. substantial amts. of Li or Mg, which break down the relatively harmless solid NaAlSix phase which exists in conventional alloys. Special attention was devoted to the Li-driven 3D(LiGa) 2D(Li2Ga) transformation that occurs at 0.02 V vs. Li+/Li on discharge.  By in situ x-ray measurements such a transition, accompanied by a 60% vol. change, is initially nicely reversible, but rapidly becomes detrimental to the cell capacity retention upon cycling.  Bypassing the (LiGa) (Li2Ga) transition considerably increases the cell performances, with capacity retention curves leveling at 300 mA h/g for at least 20 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp-qZPDsFaAs7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhY_17BhhywYQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2cXhtVSksLbF&amp;md5=59978628ad141ec18cdcdf1e53c59ab3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1149/1.3591094"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deshpande, R.
